str.<span class="pl-c1">match</span>( re );        <span class="pl-c">// [&quot;oot&quot;,&quot;ook&quot;,&quot;or&quot;]</span></pre></div><p>See how all the matches were returned at once? Sometimes that&apos;s OK, but sometimes that&apos;s not what you want.</p><p>The&#xA0;<code>y</code>&#xA0;sticky flag will give you one-at-a-time progressive matching with utilities like&#xA0;<code>test(..)</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>match(..)</code>. Just make sure the&#xA0;<code>lastIndex</code>&#xA0;is always in the right position for each match!</p><h4><a id="user-content-anchored-sticky" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#anchored-sticky" aria-hidden="true"></a>Anchored Sticky</h4><p>As we warned earlier, it&apos;s inaccurate to think of sticky mode as implying a pattern starts with&#xA0;<code>^</code>. The&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;anchor has a distinct meaning in regular expressions, which is&#xA0;<em>not altered</em>&#xA0;by sticky mode.&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;is an anchor that&#xA0;<em>always</em>&#xA0;refers to the beginning of the input, and&#xA0;<em>is not</em>&#xA0;in any way relative to&#xA0;<code>lastIndex</code>.</p><p>Besides poor/inaccurate documentation on this topic, the confusion is unfortunately strengthened further because an older pre-ES6 experiment with sticky mode in Firefox&#xA0;<em>did</em>&#xA0;make&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;relative to&#xA0;<code>lastIndex</code>, so that behavior has been around for years.</p><p>ES6 elected not to do it that way.&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;in a pattern means start-of-input absolutely and only.</p><p>As a consequence, a pattern like&#xA0;<code>/^foo/y</code>&#xA0;will always and only find a&#xA0;<code>&quot;foo&quot;</code>&#xA0;match at the beginning of a string,&#xA0;<em>if it&apos;s allowed to match there</em>. If&#xA0;<code>lastIndex</code>&#xA0;is not&#xA0;<code>0</code>, the match will fail. Consider:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> re <span class="pl-k">=</span><span class="pl-sr"> <span class="pl-pds">/</span><span class="pl-k">^</span>foo<span class="pl-pds">/</span>y</span>,
    str <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>foo<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

re.<span class="pl-c1">test</span>( str );         <span class="pl-c">// true</span>
re.<span class="pl-c1">test</span>( str );         <span class="pl-c">// false</span>
re.<span class="pl-c1">lastIndex</span>;           <span class="pl-c">// 0 -- reset after failure</span>

re.<span class="pl-c1">lastIndex</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">1</span>;
re.<span class="pl-c1">test</span>( str );         <span class="pl-c">// false -- failed for positioning</span>
re.<span class="pl-c1">lastIndex</span>;           <span class="pl-c">// 0 -- reset after failure</span></pre></div><p>Bottom line:&#xA0;<code>y</code>&#xA0;plus&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;plus&#xA0;<code>lastIndex &gt; 0</code>&#xA0;is an incompatible combination that will always cause a failed match.</p><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;While&#xA0;<code>y</code>&#xA0;does not alter the meaning of&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;in any way, the&#xA0;<code>m</code>&#xA0;multiline mode&#xA0;<em>does</em>, such that&#xA0;<code>^</code>&#xA0;means start-of-input&#xA0;<em>or</em>&#xA0;start of text after a newline. So, if you combine&#xA0;<code>y</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>m</code>&#xA0;flags together for a pattern, you can find multiple&#xA0;<code>^</code>-rooted matches in a string. But remember: because it&apos;s&#xA0;<code>y</code>&#xA0;sticky, you&apos;ll have to make sure&#xA0;<code>lastIndex</code>&#xA0;is pointing at the correct new line position (likely by matching to the end of the line) each subsequent time, or no subsequent matches will be made.</p><h3><a id="user-content-regular-expression-flags" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#regular-expression-flags" aria-hidden="true"></a>Regular Expression&#xA0;<code>flags</code></h3><p>Prior to ES6, if you wanted to examine a regular expression object to see what flags it had applied, you needed to parse them out -- ironically, probably with another regular expression -- from the content of the&#xA0;<code>source</code>&#xA0;property, such as:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> re <span class="pl-k">=</span><span class="pl-sr"> <span class="pl-pds">/</span>foo<span class="pl-pds">/</span>ig</span>;

re.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>();          <span class="pl-c">// &quot;/foo/ig&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> flags <span class="pl-k">=</span> re.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>().<span class="pl-c1">match</span>(<span class="pl-sr"> <span class="pl-pds">/</span><span class="pl-cce">\/</span>(<span class="pl-c1">[gim]</span><span class="pl-k">*</span>)<span class="pl-k">$</span><span class="pl-pds">/</span></span> )[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>];

flags;                  <span class="pl-c">// &quot;ig&quot;</span></pre></div><p>As of ES6, you can now get these values directly, with the new&#xA0;<code>flags</code>&#xA0;property:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> re <span class="pl-k">=</span><span class="pl-sr"> <span class="pl-pds">/</span>foo<span class="pl-pds">/</span>ig</span>;

re.<span class="pl-c1">flags</span>;               <span class="pl-c">// &quot;gi&quot;</span></pre></div><p>It&apos;s a small nuance, but the ES6 specification calls for the expression&apos;s flags to be listed in this order:&#xA0;<code>&quot;gimuy&quot;</code>, regardless of what order the original pattern was specified with. That&apos;s the reason for the difference between&#xA0;<code>/ig</code>&#xA0;and<code>&quot;gi&quot;</code>.</p><p>No, the order of flags specified or listed doesn&apos;t matter.</p><p>Another tweak from ES6 is that the&#xA0;<code>RegExp(..)</code>&#xA0;constructor is now&#xA0;<code>flags</code>-aware if you pass it an existing regular expression:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> re1 <span class="pl-k">=</span><span class="pl-sr"> <span class="pl-pds">/</span>foo<span class="pl-k">*</span><span class="pl-pds">/</span>y</span>;
re1.<span class="pl-c1">source</span>;                         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;foo*&quot;</span>
re1.<span class="pl-c1">flags</span>;                          <span class="pl-c">// &quot;y&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> re2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-k">new</span> <span class="pl-en">RegExp</span>( re1 );
re2.<span class="pl-c1">source</span>;                         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;foo*&quot;</span>
re2.<span class="pl-c1">flags</span>;                          <span class="pl-c">// &quot;y&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> re3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-k">new</span> <span class="pl-en">RegExp</span>( re1, <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ig<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );
re3.<span class="pl-c1">source</span>;                         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;foo*&quot;</span>
re3.<span class="pl-c1">flags</span>;                          <span class="pl-c">// &quot;gi&quot;</span></pre></div><p>Prior to ES6, the&#xA0;<code>re3</code>&#xA0;construction would throw an error, but as of ES6 you can override the flags when duplicating.</p>
### Number Literal Extensions
<h2>Number Literal Extensions</h2><p>Prior to ES5, number literals looked like the following -- the octal form was not officially specified, only allowed as an extension that browsers had come to de facto agreement on:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> dec <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>,
    oct <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">052</span>,
    hex <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0x2a</span>;</pre></div><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;Though you are specifying a number in different bases, the number&apos;s mathematic value is what is stored, and the default output interpretation is always base-10. The three variables in the previous snippet all have the&#xA0;<code>42</code>&#xA0;value stored in them.</p><p>To further illustrate that&#xA0;<code>052</code>&#xA0;was a nonstandard form extension, consider:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>42<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );             <span class="pl-c">// 42</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>052<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );            <span class="pl-c">// 52</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>0x2a<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );           <span class="pl-c">// 42</span></pre></div><p>ES5 continued to permit the browser-extended octal form (including such inconsistencies), except that in strict mode, the octal literal (<code>052</code>) form is disallowed. This restriction was done mainly because many developers had the habit (from other languages) of seemingly innocuously prefixing otherwise base-10 numbers with&#xA0;<code>0</code>&apos;s for code alignment purposes, and then running into the accidental fact that they&apos;d changed the number value entirely!</p><p>ES6 continues the legacy of changes/variations to how number literals outside base-10 numbers can be represented. There&apos;s now an official octal form, an amended hexadecimal form, and a brand-new binary form. For web compatibility reasons, the old octal&#xA0;<code>052</code>&#xA0;form will continue to be legal (though unspecified) in non-strict mode, but should really never be used anymore.</p><p>Here are the new ES6 number literal forms:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> dec <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>,
    oct <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0o52</span>,         <span class="pl-c">// or `0O52` :(</span>
    hex <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0x2a</span>,         <span class="pl-c">// or `0X2a` :/</span>
    bin <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">0b101010</span>;     <span class="pl-c">// or `0B101010` :/</span></pre></div><p>The only decimal form allowed is base-10. Octal, hexadecimal, and binary are all integer forms.</p><p>And the string representations of these forms are all able to be coerced/converted to their number equivalent:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>42<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );         <span class="pl-c">// 42</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>0o52<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );       <span class="pl-c">// 42</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>0x2a<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );       <span class="pl-c">// 42</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Number</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>0b101010<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );   <span class="pl-c">// 42</span></pre></div><p>Though not strictly new to ES6, it&apos;s a little-known fact that you can actually go the opposite direction of conversion (well, sort of):</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> a <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>;

a.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>();           <span class="pl-c">// &quot;42&quot; -- also `a.toString( 10 )`</span>
a.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">8</span> );        <span class="pl-c">// &quot;52&quot;</span>
a.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">16</span> );       <span class="pl-c">// &quot;2a&quot;</span>
a.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> );        <span class="pl-c">// &quot;101010&quot;</span></pre></div><p>In fact, you can represent a number this way in any base from&#xA0;<code>2</code>&#xA0;to&#xA0;<code>36</code>, though it&apos;d be rare that you&apos;d go outside the standard bases: 2, 8, 10, and 16.</p>
### Unicode
<h2>Unicode</h2><p>Let me just say that this section is not an exhaustive everything-you-ever-wanted-to-know-about-Unicode resource. I want to cover what you need to know that&apos;s&#xA0;<em>changing</em>&#xA0;for Unicode in ES6, but we won&apos;t go much deeper than that. Mathias Bynens (<a href="http://twitter.com/mathias">http://twitter.com/mathias</a>) has written/spoken extensively and brilliantly about JS and Unicode (see<a href="https://mathiasbynens.be/notes/javascript-unicode">https://mathiasbynens.be/notes/javascript-unicode</a>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<a href="http://fluentconf.com/javascript-html-2015/public/content/2015/02/18-javascript-loves-unicode">http://fluentconf.com/javascript-html-2015/public/content/2015/02/18-javascript-loves-unicode</a>).</p><p>The Unicode characters that range from&#xA0;<code>0x0000</code>&#xA0;to&#xA0;<code>0xFFFF</code>&#xA0;contain all the standard printed characters (in various languages) that you&apos;re likely to have seen or interacted with. This group of characters is called the&#xA0;<em>Basic Multilingual Plane (BMP)</em>. The BMP even contains fun symbols like this cool snowman: &#x2603; (U+2603).</p><p>There are lots of other extended Unicode characters beyond this BMP set, which range up to&#xA0;<code>0x10FFFF</code>. These symbols are often referred to as&#xA0;<em>astral</em>&#xA0;symbols, as that&apos;s the name given to the set of 16&#xA0;<em>planes</em>&#xA0;(e.g., layers/groupings) of characters beyond the BMP. Examples of astral symbols include &#x1D11E; (U+1D11E) and&#xA0;<g-emoji alias="hankey" fallback-src="https://assets-cdn.github.com/images/icons/emoji/unicode/1f4a9.png">&#x1F4A9;</g-emoji>&#xA0;(U+1F4A9).</p><p>Prior to ES6, JavaScript strings could specify Unicode characters using Unicode escaping, such as:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> snowman <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span><span class="pl-cce">\u</span>2603<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( snowman );         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x2603;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>However, the&#xA0;<code>\uXXXX</code>&#xA0;Unicode escaping only supports four hexadecimal characters, so you can only represent the BMP set of characters in this way. To represent an astral character using Unicode escaping prior to ES6, you need to use a<em>surrogate pair</em>&#xA0;-- basically two specially calculated Unicode-escaped characters side by side, which JS interprets together as a single astral character:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> gclef <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span><span class="pl-cce">\u</span>D834<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>DD1E<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( gclef );           <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x1D11E;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>As of ES6, we now have a new form for Unicode escaping (in strings and regular expressions), called Unicode&#xA0;<em>code point escaping</em>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> gclef <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span><span class="pl-cce">\u</span>{1D11E}<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( gclef );           <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x1D11E;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>As you can see, the difference is the presence of the&#xA0;<code>{ }</code>&#xA0;in the escape sequence, which allows it to contain any number of hexadecimal characters. Because you only need six to represent the highest possible code point value in Unicode (i.e., 0x10FFFF), this is sufficient.</p><h3><a id="user-content-unicode-aware-string-operations" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#unicode-aware-string-operations" aria-hidden="true"></a>Unicode-Aware String Operations</h3><p>By default, JavaScript string operations and methods are not sensitive to astral symbols in string values. So, they treat each BMP character individually, even the two surrogate halves that make up an otherwise single astral character. Consider:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> snowman <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>&#x2603;<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;
snowman.<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                 <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> gclef <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>&#x1D11E;<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;
gclef.<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                   <span class="pl-c">// 2</span></pre></div><p>So, how do we accurately calculate the length of such a string? In this scenario, the following trick will work:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> gclef <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>&#x1D11E;<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

[...<span class="pl-c1">gclef</span>].<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;              <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>
<span class="pl-c1">Array</span>.<span class="pl-c1">from</span>( gclef ).<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;     <span class="pl-c">// 1</span></pre></div><p>Recall from the &quot;<code>for..of</code>&#xA0;Loops&quot; section earlier in this chapter that ES6 strings have built-in iterators. This iterator happens to be Unicode-aware, meaning it will automatically output an astral symbol as a single value. We take advantage of that using the&#xA0;<code>...</code>&#xA0;spread operator in an array literal, which creates an array of the string&apos;s symbols. Then we just inspect the length of that resultant array. ES6&apos;s&#xA0;<code>Array.from(..)</code>&#xA0;does basically the same thing as&#xA0;<code>[...XYZ]</code>, but we&apos;ll cover that utility in detail in Chapter 6.</p><p><strong>Warning:</strong>&#xA0;It should be noted that constructing and exhausting an iterator just to get the length of a string is quite expensive on performance, relatively speaking, compared to what a theoretically optimized native utility/property would do.</p><p>Unfortunately, the full answer is not as simple or straightforward. In addition to the surrogate pairs (which the string iterator takes care of), there are special Unicode code points that behave in other special ways, which is much harder to account for. For example, there&apos;s a set of code points that modify the previous adjacent character, known as<em>Combining Diacritical Marks</em>.</p><p>Consider these two string outputs:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s1 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#xE9;&quot;</span>
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s2 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#xE9;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>They look the same, but they&apos;re not! Here&apos;s how we created&#xA0;<code>s1</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>s2</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span><span class="pl-cce">\x</span>E9<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>e<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;</pre></div><p>As you can probably guess, our previous&#xA0;<code>length</code>&#xA0;trick doesn&apos;t work with&#xA0;<code>s2</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre>[...<span class="pl-c1">s1</span>].<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                 <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>
[...<span class="pl-c1">s2</span>].<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                 <span class="pl-c">// 2</span></pre></div><p>So what can we do? In this case, we can perform a&#xA0;<em>Unicode normalization</em>&#xA0;on the value before inquiring about its length, using the ES6&#xA0;<code>String#normalize(..)</code>&#xA0;utility (which we&apos;ll cover more in Chapter 6):</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span><span class="pl-cce">\x</span>E9<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>e<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

s1.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;          <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>
s2.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;          <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>

s1 <span class="pl-k">===</span> s2;                      <span class="pl-c">// false</span>
s1 <span class="pl-k">===</span> s2.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>();          <span class="pl-c">// true</span></pre></div><p>Essentially,&#xA0;<code>normalize(..)</code>&#xA0;takes a sequence like&#xA0;<code>&quot;e\u0301&quot;</code>&#xA0;and normalizes it to&#xA0;<code>&quot;\xE9&quot;</code>. Normalization can even combine multiple adjacent combining marks if there&apos;s a suitable Unicode character they combine to:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>o<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0302<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0300<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> s1.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>(),
    s3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>&#x1ED3;<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

s1.<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                      <span class="pl-c">// 3</span>
s2.<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                      <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>
s3.<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;                      <span class="pl-c">// 1</span>

s2 <span class="pl-k">===</span> s3;                      <span class="pl-c">// true</span></pre></div><p>Unfortunately, normalization isn&apos;t fully perfect here, either. If you have multiple combining marks modifying a single character, you may not get the length count you&apos;d expect, because there may not be a single defined normalized character that represents the combination of all the marks. For example:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>e<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0330<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s1 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;e&#x301;&#x330;&quot;</span>

s1.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">length</span>;          <span class="pl-c">// 2</span></pre></div><p>The further you go down this rabbit hole, the more you realize that it&apos;s difficult to get one precise definition for &quot;length.&quot; What we see visually rendered as a single character -- more precisely called a&#xA0;<em>grapheme</em>&#xA0;-- doesn&apos;t always strictly relate to a single &quot;character&quot; in the program processing sense.</p><p><strong>Tip:</strong>&#xA0;If you want to see just how deep this rabbit hole goes, check out the &quot;Grapheme Cluster Boundaries&quot; algorithm (<a href="http://www.unicode.org/reports/tr29/#Grapheme_Cluster_Boundaries">http://www.Unicode.org/reports/tr29/#Grapheme_Cluster_Boundaries</a>).</p><h3><a id="user-content-character-positioning" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#character-positioning" aria-hidden="true"></a>Character Positioning</h3><p>Similar to length complications, what does it actually mean to ask, &quot;what is the character at position 2?&quot; The naive pre-ES6 answer comes from&#xA0;<code>charAt(..)</code>, which will not respect the atomicity of an astral character, nor will it take into account combining marks.</p><p>Consider:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>abc<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0107d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>{1d49e}d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s1 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;ab&#x107;d&quot;</span>
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s2 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;ab&#x107;d&quot;</span>
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( s3 );              <span class="pl-c">// &quot;ab&#x1D49E;d&quot;</span>

s1.<span class="pl-c1">charAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> );                 <span class="pl-c">// &quot;c&quot;</span>
s2.<span class="pl-c1">charAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> );                 <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>
s3.<span class="pl-c1">charAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> );                 <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&quot; &lt;-- unprintable surrogate</span>
s3.<span class="pl-c1">charAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">3</span> );                 <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&quot; &lt;-- unprintable surrogate</span></pre></div><p>So, is ES6 giving us a Unicode-aware verison of&#xA0;<code>charAt(..)</code>? Unfortunately, no. At the time of this writing, there&apos;s a proposal for such a utility that&apos;s under consideration for post-ES6.</p><p>But with what we explored in the previous section (and of course with the limitations noted thereof!), we can hack an ES6 answer:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>abc<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0107d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>{1d49e}d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

[...<span class="pl-c1">s1</span>.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>()][<span class="pl-c1">2</span>];         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>
[...<span class="pl-c1">s2</span>.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>()][<span class="pl-c1">2</span>];         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>
[...<span class="pl-c1">s3</span>.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>()][<span class="pl-c1">2</span>];         <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x1D49E;&quot;</span></pre></div><p><strong>Warning:</strong>&#xA0;Reminder of an earlier warning: constructing and exhausting an iterator each time you want to get at a single character is... very not ideal, performance wise. Let&apos;s hope we get a built-in and optimized utility for this soon, post-ES6.</p><p>What about a Unicode-aware version of the&#xA0;<code>charCodeAt(..)</code>&#xA0;utility? ES6 gives us&#xA0;<code>codePointAt(..)</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>abc<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0107d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>{1d49e}d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

s1.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ).<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">16</span> );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;107&quot;</span>

s2.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ).<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">16</span> );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;107&quot;</span>

s3.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ).<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>( <span class="pl-c1">16</span> );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;1d49e&quot;</span></pre></div><p>What about the other direction? A Unicode-aware version of&#xA0;<code>String.fromCharCode(..)</code>&#xA0;is ES6&apos;s<code>String.fromCodePoint(..)</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-c1">String</span>.<span class="pl-c1">fromCodePoint</span>( <span class="pl-c1">0x107</span> );      <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-c1">String</span>.<span class="pl-c1">fromCodePoint</span>( <span class="pl-c1">0x1d49e</span> );    <span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x1D49E;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>So wait, can we just combine&#xA0;<code>String.fromCodePoint(..)</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>codePointAt(..)</code>&#xA0;to get a better version of a Unicode-aware&#xA0;<code>charAt(..)</code>&#xA0;from earlier? Yep!</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s1 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>abc<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0301d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>0107d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    s3 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>ab<span class="pl-cce">\u</span>{1d49e}d<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>;

<span class="pl-c1">String</span>.<span class="pl-c1">fromCodePoint</span>( s1.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ) );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-c1">String</span>.<span class="pl-c1">fromCodePoint</span>( s2.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ) );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x107;&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-c1">String</span>.<span class="pl-c1">fromCodePoint</span>( s3.<span class="pl-c1">normalize</span>().<span class="pl-c1">codePointAt</span>( <span class="pl-c1">2</span> ) );
<span class="pl-c">// &quot;&#x1D49E;&quot;</span></pre></div><p>There&apos;s quite a few other string methods we haven&apos;t addressed here, including&#xA0;<code>toUpperCase()</code>,&#xA0;<code>toLowerCase()</code>,<code>substring(..)</code>,&#xA0;<code>indexOf(..)</code>,&#xA0;<code>slice(..)</code>, and a dozen others. None of these have been changed or augmented for full Unicode awareness, so you should be very careful -- probably just avoid them! -- when working with strings containing astral symbols.</p><p>There are also several string methods that use regular expressions for their behavior, like&#xA0;<code>replace(..)</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>match(..)</code>. Thankfully, ES6 brings Unicode awareness to regular expressions, as we covered in &quot;Unicode Flag&quot; earlier in this chapter.</p><p>OK, there we have it! JavaScript&apos;s Unicode string support is significantly better over pre-ES6 (though still not perfect) with the various additions we&apos;ve just covered.</p><h3><a id="user-content-unicode-identifier-names" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#unicode-identifier-names" aria-hidden="true"></a>Unicode Identifier Names</h3><p>Unicode can also be used in identifier names (variables, properties, etc.). Prior to ES6, you could do this with Unicode-escapes, like:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> \u03A9 <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>;

<span class="pl-c">// same as: var &#x3A9; = 42;</span></pre></div><p>As of ES6, you can also use the earlier explained code point escape syntax:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> \u{2B400} <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>;

<span class="pl-c">// same as: var &#x2B400; = 42;</span></pre></div><p>There&apos;s a complex set of rules around exactly which Unicode characters are allowed. Furthermore, some are allowed only if they&apos;re not the first character of the identifier name.</p><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;Mathias Bynens has a great post (<a href="https://mathiasbynens.be/notes/javascript-identifiers-es6">https://mathiasbynens.be/notes/javascript-identifiers-es6</a>) on all the nitty-gritty details.</p><p>The reasons for using such unusual characters in identifier names are rather rare and academic. You typically won&apos;t be best served by writing code that relies on these esoteric capabilities.</p>
### Symbols
<h2>Symbols</h2><p>With ES6, for the first time in quite a while, a new primitive type has been added to JavaScript: the&#xA0;<code>symbol</code>. Unlike the other primitive types, however, symbols don&apos;t have a literal form.</p><p>Here&apos;s how you create a symbol:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> sym <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">Symbol</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>some optional description<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );

<span class="pl-k">typeof</span> sym;     <span class="pl-c">// &quot;symbol&quot;</span></pre></div><p>Some things to note:</p><ul><li>You cannot and should not use&#xA0;<code>new</code>&#xA0;with&#xA0;<code>Symbol(..)</code>. It&apos;s not a constructor, nor are you producing an object.</li><li>The parameter passed to&#xA0;<code>Symbol(..)</code>&#xA0;is optional. If passed, it should be a string that gives a friendly description for the symbol&apos;s purpose.</li><li>The&#xA0;<code>typeof</code>&#xA0;output is a new value (<code>&quot;symbol&quot;</code>) that is the primary way to identify a symbol.</li></ul><p>The description, if provided, is solely used for the stringification representation of the symbol:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre>sym.<span class="pl-c1">toString</span>();     <span class="pl-c">// &quot;Symbol(some optional description)&quot;</span></pre></div><p>Similar to how primitive string values are not instances of&#xA0;<code>String</code>, symbols are also not instances of&#xA0;<code>Symbol</code>. If, for some reason, you want to construct a boxed wrapper object form of a symbol value, you can do the following:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre>sym <span class="pl-k">instanceof</span> Symbol;      <span class="pl-c">// false</span>

<span class="pl-k">var</span> symObj <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">Object</span>( sym );
symObj <span class="pl-k">instanceof</span> Symbol;   <span class="pl-c">// true</span>

symObj.<span class="pl-c1">valueOf</span>() <span class="pl-k">===</span> sym;   <span class="pl-c">// true</span></pre></div><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;<code>symObj</code>&#xA0;in this snippet is interchangeable with&#xA0;<code>sym</code>; either form can be used in all places symbols are utilized. There&apos;s not much reason to use the boxed wrapper object form (<code>symObj</code>) instead of the primitive form (<code>sym</code>). Keeping with similar advice for other primitives, it&apos;s probably best to prefer&#xA0;<code>sym</code>&#xA0;over&#xA0;<code>symObj</code>.</p><p>The internal value of a symbol itself -- referred to as its&#xA0;<code>name</code>&#xA0;-- is hidden from the code and cannot be obtained. You can think of this symbol value as an automatically generated, unique (within your application) string value.</p><p>But if the value is hidden and unobtainable, what&apos;s the point of having a symbol at all?</p><p>The main point of a symbol is to create a string-like value that can&apos;t collide with any other value. So, for example, consider using a symbol as a constant representing an event name:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-c1">EVT_LOGIN</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">Symbol</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>event.login<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );</pre></div><p>You&apos;d then use&#xA0;<code>EVT_LOGIN</code>&#xA0;in place of a generic string literal like&#xA0;<code>&quot;event.login&quot;</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre>evthub.<span class="pl-c1">listen</span>( <span class="pl-c1">EVT_LOGIN</span>, <span class="pl-k">function</span>(<span class="pl-smi">data</span>){
    <span class="pl-c">// ..</span>
} );</pre></div><p>The benefit here is that&#xA0;<code>EVT_LOGIN</code>&#xA0;holds a value that cannot be duplicated (accidentally or otherwise) by any other value, so it is impossible for there to be any confusion of which event is being dispatched or handled.</p><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;Under the covers, the&#xA0;<code>evthub</code>&#xA0;utility assumed in the previous snippet would almost certainly be using the symbol value from the&#xA0;<code>EVT_LOGIN</code>&#xA0;argument directly as the property/key in some internal object (hash) that tracks event handlers. If&#xA0;<code>evthub</code>&#xA0;instead needed to use the symbol value as a real string, it would need to explicitly coerce with<code>String(..)</code>&#xA0;or&#xA0;<code>toString()</code>, as implicit string coercion of symbols is not allowed.</p><p>You may use a symbol directly as a property name/key in an object, such as a special property that you want to treat as hidden or meta in usage. It&apos;s important to know that although you intend to treat it as such, it is not&#xA0;<em>actually</em>&#xA0;a hidden or untouchable property.</p><p>Consider this module that implements the&#xA0;<em>singleton</em>&#xA0;pattern behavior -- that is, it only allows itself to be created once:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">Symbol</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>instance<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );

<span class="pl-k">function</span> <span class="pl-en">HappyFace</span>() {
    <span class="pl-k">if</span> (HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>]) <span class="pl-k">return</span> HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>];

    <span class="pl-k">function</span> <span class="pl-en">smile</span>() { .. }

    <span class="pl-k">return</span> HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>] <span class="pl-k">=</span> {
        smile<span class="pl-k">:</span> smile
    };
}

<span class="pl-k">var</span> me <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">HappyFace</span>(),
    you <span class="pl-k">=</span> <span class="pl-c1">HappyFace</span>();

me <span class="pl-k">===</span> you;         <span class="pl-c">// true</span></pre></div><p>The&#xA0;<code>INSTANCE</code>&#xA0;symbol value here is a special, almost hidden, meta-like property stored statically on the&#xA0;<code>HappyFace()</code>function object.</p><p>It could alternatively have been a plain old property like&#xA0;<code>__instance</code>, and the behavior would have been identical. The usage of a symbol simply improves the metaprogramming style, keeping this&#xA0;<code>INSTANCE</code>&#xA0;property set apart from any other normal properties.</p><h3><a id="user-content-symbol-registry" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#symbol-registry" aria-hidden="true"></a>Symbol Registry</h3><p>One mild downside to using symbols as in the last few examples is that the&#xA0;<code>EVT_LOGIN</code>&#xA0;and&#xA0;<code>INSTANCE</code>&#xA0;variables had to be stored in an outer scope (perhaps even the global scope), or otherwise somehow stored in a publicly available location, so that all parts of the code that need to use the symbols can access them.</p><p>To aid in organizing code with access to these symbols, you can create symbol values with the&#xA0;<em>global symbol registry</em>. For example:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-c1">EVT_LOGIN</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>event.login<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );

<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( <span class="pl-c1">EVT_LOGIN</span> );       <span class="pl-c">// Symbol(event.login)</span></pre></div><p>And:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">function</span> <span class="pl-en">HappyFace</span>() {
    <span class="pl-k">const</span> <span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span> <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>instance<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );

    <span class="pl-k">if</span> (HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>]) <span class="pl-k">return</span> HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>];

    <span class="pl-c">// ..</span>

    <span class="pl-k">return</span> HappyFace[<span class="pl-c1">INSTANCE</span>] <span class="pl-k">=</span> { .. };
}</pre></div><p><code>Symbol.for(..)</code>&#xA0;looks in the global symbol registry to see if a symbol is already stored with the provided description text, and returns it if so. If not, it creates one to return. In other words, the global symbol registry treats symbol values, by description text, as singletons themselves.</p><p>But that also means that any part of your application can retrieve the symbol from the registry using&#xA0;<code>Symbol.for(..)</code>, as long as the matching description name is used.</p><p>Ironically, symbols are basically intended to replace the use of&#xA0;<em>magic strings</em>&#xA0;(arbitrary string values given special meaning) in your application. But you precisely use&#xA0;<em>magic</em>&#xA0;description string values to uniquely identify/locate them in the global symbol registry!</p><p>To avoid accidental collisions, you&apos;ll probably want to make your symbol descriptions quite unique. One easy way of doing that is to include prefix/context/namespacing information in them.</p><p>For example, consider a utility such as the following:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">function</span> <span class="pl-en">extractValues</span>(<span class="pl-smi">str</span>) {
    <span class="pl-k">var</span> key <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>extractValues.parse<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> ),
        re <span class="pl-k">=</span> extractValues[key] <span class="pl-k">||</span>
<span class="pl-sr">            <span class="pl-pds">/</span><span class="pl-c1">[<span class="pl-k">^</span>=&amp;]</span><span class="pl-k">+?</span>=(<span class="pl-c1">[<span class="pl-k">^</span>&amp;]</span><span class="pl-k">+?</span>)(?=&amp;<span class="pl-k">|</span><span class="pl-k">$</span>)<span class="pl-pds">/</span>g</span>,
        values <span class="pl-k">=</span> [], match;

    <span class="pl-k">while</span> (match <span class="pl-k">=</span> re.<span class="pl-c1">exec</span>( str )) {
        values.<span class="pl-c1">push</span>( match[<span class="pl-c1">1</span>] );
    }

    <span class="pl-k">return</span> values;
}</pre></div><p>We use the magic string value&#xA0;<code>&quot;extractValues.parse&quot;</code>&#xA0;because it&apos;s quite unlikely that any other symbol in the registry would ever collide with that description.</p><p>If a user of this utility wants to override the parsing regular expression, they can also use the symbol registry:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre>extractValues[Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>extractValues.parse<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> )] <span class="pl-k">=</span>
<span class="pl-sr">    <span class="pl-pds">/</span><span class="pl-c1">..</span>some pattern<span class="pl-c1">..</span><span class="pl-pds">/</span>g</span>;

<span class="pl-c1">extractValues</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>..some string..<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );</pre></div><p>Aside from the assistance the symbol registry provides in globally storing these values, everything we&apos;re seeing here could have been done by just actually using the magic string&#xA0;<code>&quot;extractValues.parse&quot;</code>&#xA0;as the key, rather than the symbol. The improvements exist at the metaprogramming level more than the functional level.</p><p>You may have occasion to use a symbol value that has been stored in the registry to look up what description text (key) it&apos;s stored under. For example, you may need to signal to another part of your application how to locate a symbol in the registry because you cannot pass the symbol value itself.</p><p>You can retrieve a registered symbol&apos;s description text (key) using&#xA0;<code>Symbol.keyFor(..)</code>:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> s <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>something cool<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> );

<span class="pl-k">var</span> desc <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">keyFor</span>( s );
<span class="pl-en">console</span><span class="pl-c1">.log</span>( desc );            <span class="pl-c">// &quot;something cool&quot;</span>

<span class="pl-c">// get the symbol from the registry again</span>
<span class="pl-k">var</span> s2 <span class="pl-k">=</span> Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">for</span>( desc );

s2 <span class="pl-k">===</span> s;                       <span class="pl-c">// true</span></pre></div><h3><a id="user-content-symbols-as-object-properties" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#symbols-as-object-properties" aria-hidden="true"></a>Symbols as Object Properties</h3><p>If a symbol is used as a property/key of an object, it&apos;s stored in a special way so that the property will not show up in a normal enumeration of the object&apos;s properties:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> o <span class="pl-k">=</span> {
    foo<span class="pl-k">:</span> <span class="pl-c1">42</span>,
    [ <span class="pl-c1">Symbol</span>( <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>bar<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span> ) ]<span class="pl-k">:</span> <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span>hello world<span class="pl-pds">&quot;</span></span>,
    baz<span class="pl-k">:</span> <span class="pl-c1">true</span>
};

<span class="pl-c1">Object</span>.<span class="pl-c1">getOwnPropertyNames</span>( o );    <span class="pl-c">// [ &quot;foo&quot;,&quot;baz&quot; ]</span></pre></div><p>To retrieve an object&apos;s symbol properties:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-c1">Object</span>.<span class="pl-c1">getOwnPropertySymbols</span>( o );  <span class="pl-c">// [ Symbol(bar) ]</span></pre></div><p>This makes it clear that a property symbol is not actually hidden or inaccessible, as you can always see it in the<code>Object.getOwnPropertySymbols(..)</code>&#xA0;list.</p><h4><a id="user-content-built-in-symbols" class="anchor" href="https://github.com/getify/You-Dont-Know-JS/blob/master/es6%20&amp;%20beyond/ch2.md#built-in-symbols" aria-hidden="true"></a>Built-In Symbols</h4><p>ES6 comes with a number of predefined built-in symbols that expose various meta behaviors on JavaScript object values. However, these symbols are&#xA0;<em>not</em>&#xA0;registered in the global symbol registry, as one might expect.</p><p>Instead, they&apos;re stored as properties on the&#xA0;<code>Symbol</code>&#xA0;function object. For example, in the &quot;<code>for..of</code>&quot; section earlier in this chapter, we introduced the&#xA0;<code>Symbol.iterator</code>&#xA0;value:</p><div class="highlight highlight-js"><pre><span class="pl-k">var</span> a <span class="pl-k">=</span> [<span class="pl-c1">1</span>,<span class="pl-c1">2</span>,<span class="pl-c1">3</span>];

a[Symbol.<span class="pl-c1">iterator</span>];         <span class="pl-c">// native function</span></pre></div><p>The specification uses the&#xA0;<code>@@</code>&#xA0;prefix notation to refer to the built-in symbols, the most common ones being:<code>@@iterator</code>,&#xA0;<code>@@toStringTag</code>,&#xA0;<code>@@toPrimitive</code>. Several others are defined as well, though they probably won&apos;t be used as often.</p><p><strong>Note:</strong>&#xA0;See &quot;Well Known Symbols&quot; in Chapter 7 for detailed information about how these built-in symbols are used for meta programming purposes.</p>
#### String value that can't be repeated
### Review
<h2>Review</h2><p>ES6 adds a heap of new syntax forms to JavaScript, so there&apos;s plenty to learn!</p><p>Most of these are designed to ease the pain points of common programming idioms, such as setting default values to function parameters and gathering the &quot;rest&quot; of the parameters into an array. Destructuring is a powerful tool for more concisely expressing assignments of values from arrays and nested objects.</p><p>While features like&#xA0;<code>=&gt;</code>&#xA0;arrow functions appear to also be all about shorter and nicer-looking syntax, they actually have very specific behaviors that you should intentionally use only in appropriate situations.</p><p>Expanded Unicode support, new tricks for regular expressions, and even a new primitive&#xA0;<code>symbol</code>&#xA0;type round out the syntactic evolution of ES6.</p>
